  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/743487180" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/24778968-spencer-johnston" class="left imgcol" title="Spencer Johnston"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" alt="Spencer Johnston" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/743487180?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 16, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/24778968-spencer-johnston" class="user" title="Spencer Johnston">Spencer Johnston</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/38709.Holes" title="Paperback 9780439244190" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer743487180">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15926004772414703662"> Holes by Louis Sachar is a mystery/adventure novel about a boy named Stanley Yelnats. Stanley was from an economically disadvantaged family. He was accused of a theft that he did not commit. At his trial, Stanley is found guilty and has a choice of going to jail or a juvenile work prison called Camp Green Lake. He thinks carefully and decides on giving Camp Green Lake a chance.<br /><br />The majority of the book is about Stanley's hard labor at Green Lake, the mystery surrounding Green Lake and Stanley's</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15926004772414703662"> Holes by Louis Sachar is a mystery/adventure novel about a boy named Stanley Yelnats. Stanley was from an economically disadvantaged family. He was accused of a theft that he did not commit. At his trial, Stanley is found guilty and has a choice of going to jail or a juvenile work prison called Camp Green Lake. He thinks carefully and decides on giving Camp Green Lake a chance.<br /><br />The majority of the book is about Stanley's hard labor at Green Lake, the mystery surrounding Green Lake and Stanley's path to discovering the truth behind who committed the crime he was accused of doing. The book takes place at an old dried up lake that had always been hot and not green at all. According to the warden, it is believed that it had not rained at Camp Green Lake in over 100 years. Stanley's expectations of Camp Green Lake were not at all what he thought it would be. He expected to be required to work hard at Camp but never imagined that he would work hard labor all day, seven days a week. The title of the book gets its name because of the activity the juvenile inmates do at Camp Green Lake. They are required to dig 8 foot deep holes. The length of the work day depends on how long it takes them to dig their hole. Stanley often wonders what they are looking for while digging all of these holes. But, his counselor and the warden tell him that they are not looking for anything in the holes. It is up to Stanley to figure out.<br /><br />Louis Sachar does a great job of holding the mystery all through out the novel. The mystery behind this crime will keep you guessing and once you begin reading the book you will never want to stop. This is both a mystery and suspense novel. Sachar does a good job of always making sure there is conflict in the story. The author resolves the conflict when you least expect it and the end result is always surprising. The themes of this book include good versus evil, the underdog beating the odds and the power of friendship. <br /><br />My overall review of this book is excellent. The author's choices in this novel are excellent. The characters are so interesting that I found myself forgetting that the characters were make believe. The description of Camp Green Lake was so detailed that I could imagine it in my mind. Holes was a very entertaining book that is suitable from middle school to high school readers. The plot is interesting without being overly complex so it is perfect for any reader. The suspense keeps the reader on the edge of their seat the entire time they are reading this novel. This book even covers the history of Camp Green Lake and builds all the way to an exciting conclusion. It also a book that encourages the reader to remain hopeful when faced with difficulty in life. If you are looking for a great mystery, adventure, suspense book, then Holes is a great book that includes all of them. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15926004772414703662">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16663539129039377466">Great little book! If my future children are readers, this is definitely one I'd like to set in front of them. <br /><br />The main character, Stanley Yelnats, is wrongly accused of stealing a pair of sneakers. And when it comes right down to it, he's not all that surprised that something like that would have happened to him thanks to a generational curse that has affected his family going all the way back to a promise broken by his great-great grandfather. Through an unusual set of circumstances (call it destiny?) Stanley is able to write that wrong more than one hundred years later. <br /><br />Stanley is an overweight kid who doesn't have a lot of friends. His family is struggling financially but through it all, he is still a conscientious character who overcomes all his past struggles and is awarded with a very fulfilling (albeit coincidentally) happy ending. <br /><br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2325061289432579696">I just started Holes by Louis Sachar and the book is about a boy named Stanley who goes to sort of like a delinquent camp where as a punishment he has to dig a hole in the hot, grueling sun every single day that he is at the camp.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText2325061289432579696">I just started Holes by Louis Sachar and the book is about a boy named Stanley who goes to sort of like a delinquent camp where as a punishment he has to dig a hole in the hot, grueling sun every single day that he is at the camp. At first it took me a little while to get interested in the book, but when I got interested the book just basically sucked me in. One thing that made the book so great was how descriptive Sachar was. For example, when he gets his shovel for the first time he describes as strong and hard against his soft, fleshy skin. One of the reasons that the great description made the book even better is because I can really picture what is happening and what everything looks like. I love this book so far and I think that it is definitely a good read. </span>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5343137282153236258">I have been meaning to pick this book up ever since I watched the movie as I kid, and I am glad that I finally picked it up. It was an easy read that I flew through in only an hour or so. The characters were realistic, though a little dull. I loved the flashbacks to Kate Barlow's story, seeing how the "curse" came to be and the history of Green Lake. The ending was terrible, but kind of hilarious at the same time.<br /><br />Overall, a funny, easy read that I would certainly recommend. </span>

            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4063913-beth?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4063913-beth?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4063913-beth?shelf=project-nostalgia" class="actionLinkLite">project-nostalgia</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer113620616">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5371178274649572582"><em>The yellow-spotted lizards... eat small animals, insects, certain cactus thorns, and the shells of sunflower seeds.</em><br /><br />This, casually mentioned just after someone spat shells into freshly dug holes. Because he'd taken up sunflower seeds after quitting smoking. Which the Warden did not appreciate.<br /><br />It's the structure of <em>Holes</em> that's always stood out to me. The way the details are revealed - and then tied into seemingly unrelated, already-mentioned details - and the way it builds on itself - it's nothin</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5371178274649572582"><em>The yellow-spotted lizards... eat small animals, insects, certain cactus thorns, and the shells of sunflower seeds.</em><br /><br />This, casually mentioned just after someone spat shells into freshly dug holes. Because he'd taken up sunflower seeds after quitting smoking. Which the Warden did not appreciate.<br /><br />It's the structure of <em>Holes</em> that's always stood out to me. The way the details are revealed - and then tied into seemingly unrelated, already-mentioned details - and the way it builds on itself - it's nothing short of outstanding. <em>Holes</em> is a puzzle, and I love seeing how it all slots together. What's more, I still remember what it felt like, putting it all together for the first time. <br /><br />Sometimes Newbery books strike me as adult-books and not necessarily as books kids themselves would choose. This book, though - I remember reading this as a kid, right when it came out; I think <em>Holes</em> is one of those novels that are immediately apparent as child-pleasers to adults, even as adults appreciate it for themselves. <br /><br />This is something I've only thought about on this reread: I like the way the nail polish is used. I like it a lot. It reminds me of the way <em>Crown Duel</em>'s court used fans to communicate under their tyrant's nose, and he persisted in disregarding them because fans were feminine. The Warden doesn't quite have that - shall we say - subtlety, but the unexpectedness is so refreshing. I suppose a more apt comparison would be Yuki and the Yamani fans in Tamora Pierce's Protector of the Small series - "Beware the women of the warrior class, for all they touch is both decorative and deadly." Obviously, I'm not advocating torture-by-nail-polish. But there's a facet here that speaks to me.<br /><br />There's a lot going on in <em>Holes,</em> and all of it works. And the novel manages to pull all of it off without looking like it's doing any heavy lifting. It's a brilliantly paced and structured balancing act. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5371178274649572582">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
